<p>7 AP-fondernas investeringsprocess</p>
<p>I avsnitt 7.1 presenteras en sammanfattning (i punktform) av Mercers syn på AP-<br>fondernas investeringsprocess. Investeringsprocessen har utvärderats av Pamela<br>Garrud och Bill Sykes. Det är i huvudsak intervjumöten med respektive<br>fondstyrelse som ligger till grund för Mercers utvärdering i denna del.<br>Kompletterande information har insamlats telefonledes.</p>
<p>Anteckningar från intervjumöten presenteras i avsnitt 7.2-7.5 för respektive<br>fondorganisation.</p>
<p>Under avsnitt 7.2.6 samt 7.2.7 har “sekretessbelagd information” angivits. Detta<br>innebär att avsnittet har utgått pga att rapporten i denna del bygger på uppgifter<br>som erhållits från fondstyrelserna och för vilka enligt styrelserna sekretess enligt 6<br>kap. Sekretesslagen(1980:100) gäller.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>85</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärderine av första. andra, tredie. fiärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltnim 799S-7997</p>
<p>7.1 BULLET POINTS OF THE INVESTMENTPROCESS OF THE AP-FUNDS</p>
<p>First - Third Funds</p>
<p>Overall View</p>
<p>■ The two large and striking issues which face the First through Third Funds are:</p>
<p>• the unwieldy board structure and;</p>
<p>■ the difficulties inherent in managing a portfolio which represents 24% of<br>the country’s outstanding<sup>1</sup> debt markets.</p>
<p>■ The board/s should be restructured and an “optimal” membership must be<br>considered. We believe that some representation on the board from the<br>financial community is necessary for proper oversight.</p>
<p>■ There is a need for a benchmark which adequately measures the Funds’<br>investible universe.</p>
<p>• Such a benchmark should be calculated and maintained by a third party.</p>
<p>• The head of fixed income has a very long market experience in Swedish fixed<br>income and seems to be running the team in a professional manner.</p>
<p>• The domestic fixed income team appear adequately resourced, particularly in<br>comparison to the equity funds which are not.</p>
<p>■ We question whether anv fund management organisation can adequately<br>manage non-domestic fixed income portfolios with only two individuals and<br>four analysts. Outsourcing may be a better solution.</p>
<p>Problems to be Addressed</p>
<p>1. Structure of the Board/s</p>
<p>• With 14 Board members each and 14 deputies, we question the efficiency of the<br>boards’ decision-making process.</p>
<p>■ With 3 separate Boards and 3 separate board meetings and the fact that all must<br>agree to every action, we question whether there is any true oversight occurring<br>and where accountability actively resides.</p>
<p>• We believe that, if the three funds decide to remain as one, there should be one<br>board going forward, the number of Board members should be smaller, and<br>some members should be financial market professionals.</p>
<p>■ The above is not a criticism of either Mr. Nilsson or of the Investment<br>Committee. However, we believe that the board needs to make an active effort<br>to provide true oversight of the Funds, since it bears the oversight<br>responsibility.</p>
<p>2. Benchmark for Swedish Fixed Income</p>
<p>■ Given the very large size of the First through Third Funds (which manage about<br>24% of Sweden’s outstanding debt market), there is a need for a benchmark<br>which enables both the fund and the board/s to measure risk and retum in an<br>objective manner.</p>
<p>• The Funds have attempted to construct intemally a benchmark which<br>encompasses the majority of the private and public debt outstanding in the<br>market.</p>
<p>' We use the term “outstanding debt märket&quot; to refer to nominal and index-linked govemment debt<br>as weil as privately-issued Swedish debt.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>86</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredje, fiärde, femte ocb sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>• The commonly-used OMRX indices are inadequate for measuring the<br>performance of a fund this size due to: a) the fact that they exclude the private<br>placement market and b) due to their division into indices representing maturity<br>sectors.</p>
<p>■ The inadequacies of the indices have resulted in the Funds using an absolute<br>duration benchmark which means that both risk and performance are not<br>measured effectively.</p>
<p>■ One suggestion would be to construct a govemment bond index encompassing<br>all maturity sectors against which the private placement issues could be<br>managed on a spread basis. This index should be constructed and measured by<br>a third party. We believe that this proposed index is close the manner in which<br>the funds are being managed in any event.</p>
<p>• We do not believe that the Funds are taking undue risk. However, over the<br>long term, it is necessary that both the boards and the investment managers<br>have a clear idea of what is expected of performance so that both may measure<br>whether risk and return have been measured effectively.</p>
<p>3. What Kind of Performance Can be Realistically Expected of a Very Large<br>Fund?</p>
<p>■ We know from practical experience that it is extremely difficult to outperform<br>the market when monies under management are large. However, very little<br>measurement work has been undertaken to quantify the effect of size.</p>
<p>• The Funds’ portfolios have a short duration (in absolute terms and relative to<br>other bond markets) and we believe that a lot of the repositioning of the<br>portfolio is done when redemptions are made, so a structural disadvantage of<br>the magnitude suggested above cannot necessarily be inferred.</p>
<p>■ Nonetheless, it is reasonable to assume that, over the long term, this portfolio<br>would do very well to equal or better the performance of the domestic market.</p>
<p>Issues to be Considered</p>
<p>1. Using Government Assets to Pay off Government Debt</p>
<p>■ One is struck by the obvious fact that thé govemment, via the First through<br>Third Funds, owns about 25% of the outstanding govemment debt.</p>
<p>■ One option to be considered is the obvious one of using some of the<br>governments assets to pay off this debt.</p>
<p>• It should be pointed out that most Anglo-Saxon systems would be<br>philosophically opposed to using pension fund assets to pay off govemment<br>debt.</p>
<p>■ Pension fund assets would be regarded by the Anglo-Saxon systems as having<br>been set aside to meet the obligations of future pension liabilities, not the<br>liabilities associated with issued govemment debt.</p>
<p>2. A Core/Satellite Asset Management Structure</p>
<p>■ Many pension funds which represent a significant portion of their domestic<br>equity or debt markets consider the option of having a passive core manager<br>and an active satellite manager.</p>
<p>■ The passive manager would be expected to replicate the index performance<br>while the active manager would be expected to outperform the index with a<br>significantlv smaller amount of assets.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>87</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredje, fiärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>■ One possible solution is to have the in-house managers replicate the index and,<br>perhaps, one in-house “satellite” team with other, extemal satellite managers.</p>
<p>■ With no denigration of the existing team (because they can do nothing else),<br>they are already acting as quasi-index managers (even if the exact composition<br>of the index is not knowni).</p>
<p>3. Outsourcing International Fixed Income Management</p>
<p>■ Outsourcing non-Swedish fixed income management is an obvious role for an<br>extemal, satellite manager.</p>
<p>■ We question whether anv fund management organisation can adequately<br>manage non-domestic fixed income portfolios with only two individuals.</p>
<p>Fourth &amp; Fifth Fund - General Equity Issues</p>
<p>■ Combined equity assets of the Fourth and Fifth Fund are about SKr. 115 billion,<br>representing over 4.5% of the domestic equity market. This presents certain<br>built-in performance problems, especially for the Fourth Fund.</p>
<p>■ The Funds have an information advantage in terms of access to the management<br>of Swedish companies. This is an important advantage which the Funds have<br>been able to exploit.</p>
<p>• The Funds do not enjoy an information advantage in non-Swedish markets, a<br>fact that they are acutely aware of with the coming of the Euro and increased<br>harmonisation of European equity markets. This argues either for a rapid<br>development of this expertise in-house, or for the out-sourcing of non-Swedish<br>equity management.</p>
<p>■ Although some large Swedish commercial banks probably also have an<br>information advantage (due to size, not due to inside information), anecdotal<br>evidence suggests that the AP Funds still enjoy greater management access.</p>
<p>■ We do not believe that the Funds currently have enough human resources to<br>manage both domestic and intemational equities. They also do not have<br>sufficient experience as currently constituted to manage non-Swedish equities.</p>
<p>■ We would argue that the diversification into non-Swedish equity markets is vital<br>if the overall assets of the system are going to quadruple in relative size over the<br>coming years.</p>
<p>Issues to be Considered</p>
<p>1. One Unified Approach to Equity Investing</p>
<p>• Under the current approach, equity investments are divided up between the<br>Fourth Fund (SKr. 100 billion) and the Fifth Fund (SKr. 15 billion) with each<br>board allowed to take the strategic approach that they see fit.</p>
<p>■ There is an argument for taking a “unified” strategic approach toward the entire<br>equity strategy, even if this results subsequently in equity assets being divided<br>into different geographic or risk-adjusted mandates.</p>
<p>■ A core/satellite asset management structure as described on the section on fixed<br>income should also be considered within Swedish equities.</p>
<p>2. Overseas Equities as a Strategic Holding</p>
<p>■ We believe that there is a strong case for considering non-Swedish equities as<br>an integral part of the overall asset strategy although this should be part of a full<br>strategy review.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>88</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredie, fiärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>* By investing outside of the domestic market, the Funds can reduce risk without<br>sacrificing long-term return.</p>
<p>■ Risk reduction possibilities include: diversification out of Sweden-specific risks<br>(interest rates, fiscal/tax policy) and broader diversification among industries<br>and individual companies.</p>
<p>* Potential return enhancement includes a greater pool of investment<br>opportunities and more flexibility of portfolio management without adversely<br>impacting market prices.</p>
<p>Fourth Fund</p>
<p>Overall View</p>
<p>■ The fourth fund shares its most striking issue with the First through Third and<br>the Fifth funds, namely its unwieldy board structure.</p>
<p>■ The board should be restructured and an “optimal” membership must be<br>considered. We believe that some representation on the board from the<br>financial community is necessary for proper oversight.</p>
<p>■ The investment professionals appear to be well qualified and all, including the<br>Managing Director, have previous experience in the financial markets.</p>
<p>■ Nonetheless, there is probably still an argument that the team of investment<br>professionals ought to be somewhat larger in order to manage the amount of<br>assets they have under management.</p>
<p>* The Fourth Fund’s performance benchmark, while more challenging than the<br>Fifth Fund’s, probably needs to be even more precise although care must be<br>taken to make certain it is achievable.</p>
<p>* A fund of this size, which represents about 3.5% of the domestic equity market,<br>will be at a significant disadvantage vis-å-vis performance due to its share of the<br>market.</p>
<p>■ The information advantage with the Fund enjoys due to its unrivalled access to<br>company management is a significant competitive advantage. The Fund is<br>aware of this and uses the advantage in a proactive and responsible manner<br>(e.g. encouraging more efficient use of equity).</p>
<p>Problems to be Addressed</p>
<p>1. Structure of the Board</p>
<p>■ With 14 Board members each and 14 deputies, we question the efficiency of the<br>board’s decision-making process.</p>
<p>* We believe that the number of Board members should be smaller, and some<br>members should be financial market professionals.</p>
<p>■ The above is not a criticism of either Mr. Halvorsen or of the Investment<br>Committee. However, we believe that the board needs to make an active effort<br>to provide true oversight of the Funds, since it bears the oversight<br>responsibility.</p>
<p>2. Issues Surrounding the Benchmark &amp; Size of Fund</p>
<p>■ The Fourth Fund has adopted the Findata index both as its risk benchmark and<br>its performance benchmark.</p>
<p>* The top five holdings represent about 50% of the total domestic portfolio and<br>the top 18 holdings represent about 87% of the domestic portfolio.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>89</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Ulvärderine av första, andra, tredie. Iförde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvallntne 1991-1997</p>
<p>■ The Fourth Fund holds about 17% of the market capitalisation of Ericsson, 15%<br>Astra, 7% ABB, 6% Volvo and 5% Sandvik.</p>
<p>■ While a significant part of this concentration in holdings is due to the<br>composition of the index, it vividly illustrates the need for such a large fund to<br>diversify internationally.</p>
<p>■ The Fund will not be able to change its domestic portfolio strategy over the<br>short term (due to a negative effect on market prices) and therefore, the chances<br>of outperforming the domestic index are significantly reduced.</p>
<p>3. What Kind of Performance Can be Realistically Expected of a Very Large<br>Fund?</p>
<p>■ We know from practical experience that it is extremely difficult to outperform<br>the market when monies under management are large. However, very little<br>measurement work has been undertaken to quantify the effect of size.</p>
<p>■ For illustrative purposes only, one study in another relatively small equity<br>market suggests that when funds being managed reach 3.5% of the market or<br>more, they have an initial all-in cost of close to 2.5%, i.e. performance might be<br>expected to be 2.5% below the index.</p>
<p>■ We cannot necessarily assume such costs for the Fourth Fund, particularly as the<br>level of tumover in the Fund is much lower than assumed in the study above.</p>
<p>• However, with about 5.5% of the domestic market under management, it is stili<br>reasonable to assume that the Fourth Fund would encounter a performance<br>disadvantage due to size, although this cannot be precisely quantified.</p>
<p>Issues to be Considered</p>
<p>1. Size of the Team</p>
<p>• With five investment professionals (the sixth was leaving the week we visited)<br>and three non-investment staff, the Fund is probably understaffed.</p>
<p>• As a very rough estimate, the ratio of costs to funds under management in a<br>commercial operation (excluding marketing costs) for a fund this size would be<br>about 20 basis points (0.20%). Fund management and research would cost<br>about 5 basis points (0.05%). This cost level would imply a total staff budget of<br>SKr. 50 million for SKr. 100 billion under management.</p>
<p>Fifth Fund</p>
<p>Overall View</p>
<p>■ The two striking issues which face the Fifth Fund are:</p>
<p>■ the unwieldy board structure and;</p>
<p>&quot; the small size of the team.</p>
<p>• The board must be restructured and an “optimal” membership must be<br>considered. We believe that some representation on the board from the<br>financial community is necessary for proper oversight.</p>
<p>■ Although the two investment professionals are qualified, we do not believe that<br>two is a sufficient number to manage a domestic equity portfolio of this size<br>according to best practice.</p>
<p>• The Fifth Fund’s performance benchmark is too vague. A performance<br>benchmark should probably be set against the domestic index although this<br>fund will still suffer from a “size effect” and this should be taken into account.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>90</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredie. fiärde. femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>Nonetheless, the target outperformance benchmark which is inflation-oriented<br>rather than market-oriented is not relevant and is probably an easy benchmark<br>to beat over the longer-term (although not necessarily in any given individual<br>year).</p>
<p>■ The Fund holds the view that is possible to identify good companies but that it<br>is not possible to judge their worth in the context of the equity markets. We<br>disagree.</p>
<p>■ By limiting the number of stocks which may be held to twenty or twenty five,<br>the Board has sought to limit risk by trying to ensure that companies invested in<br>were well known; in fact, it has increased stock-specific risk in the portfolio by<br>not allowing for appropriate company diversification.</p>
<p>■ The practice of investing in overseas equities against a domestic benchmark<br>(e.g. purchasing Roche instead of Astra) is common-place in many markets but,<br>nonetheless, questionable. This practice makes benchmarking difficult and<br>obscures the fact that foreign holdings should be a part of strategy with the<br>country and market risk taken into account in the overall fund structure.</p>
<p>• The information advantage which the Fund enjoys due to its unrivalled access to<br>company management is a significant competitive advantage. The Fund is<br>aware of this and, like the Fourth fund, uses the advantage in a proactive<br>manner.</p>
<p>Problems to be Addressed</p>
<p>1. Structure of the Board</p>
<p>• With 14 Board members each and 14 deputies, we question the efficiency of the<br>board’s decision-making process.</p>
<p>■ We believe that the number of Board members should be smaller, and some<br>members should be financial market professionals.</p>
<p>• The above is not a criticism of either Mr. Låftman or of the Investment<br>Committee. However, we believe that the board needs to make an active effort<br>to provide true oversight of the Funds, since it bears the oversight<br>responsibility.</p>
<p>2. Size of the Team</p>
<p>• In our view, there are effectively two investment professionals at this fund,<br>which is not sufficient.</p>
<p>• The Managing Director is an esteemed and experienced businessman with<br>excellent company contacts and good knowledge of management and business<br>practices, but he is not an investment professional.</p>
<p>■ As a very rough estimate, the ratio of costs to funds under management in a<br>commercial operation (excluding marketing costs) for a fund this size would be<br>about 22 basis points (0.22%). Fund management and research would cost<br>about 10 basis points (0.10%). This cost level would imply a total staff budget<br>of SKr. 15 million for SKr. 15 billion under management.</p>
<p>■ Along with the size of the investment team, the size of the support team is<br>probably at the bare minimum as well.</p>
<p>3. Benchmark</p>
<p>■ There is no reason why the Fifth Fund, given the current structure, could not<br>benchmark its performance against a commonly used index such as the Findata<br>Index.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>91</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredie, fiärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>• With SKr. 15 billion under management, the Fund still represents about 0.9% of<br>the domestic market capitalisation, so there will be a negative effect on<br>performance from size, and this should be taken into account.</p>
<p>■ Although very little measurement work has been undertaken to quantify the<br>effect of size, one study in another relatively small equity market suggests that<br>when funds being managed reach 1% of the market capitalisation, they have an<br>initial all-in cost of about 1.5%.</p>
<p>■ As a direct “translation” of these costs cannot be inferred, the most that we can<br>assume is that the size of the Fifth Fund will contribute to a performance<br>disadvantage, although this will be somewhat moderated by<sup>7</sup> the Fund’s low<br>tumover.</p>
<p>• Based on rough experience, this cost effect implies that “index minus” rather<br>than “index plus” might be an appropriate target. However, the current<br>inflation-linked benchmark is nonetheless, far too easily achievable over the<br>long term.</p>
<p>■ The size disadvantage also supports the idea of a core/satellite structure.</p>
<p>4. Investment Philosophy</p>
<p>■ The Fund holds the view, and practices the investment philosophy, that it is<br>possible to identify good long-term growth companies but that the market’s<br>valuation of these companies is ephemeral, possible irrational. Therefore the<br>Fund seeks simply to buy and hold good long-term growth companies.</p>
<p>• While many a good investment manager seeks to buy and hold long-term<br>growth companies, part of being a good investment manager is having the<br>ability to buy the stocks of these companies at good valuations. It is indeed<br>possible to buy good companies at bad prices and to have performance suffer<br>as a result for a significant period of time.</p>
<p>■ We disagree with the view that it is not possible to value companies and we do<br>not believe that the markets valuation is irrational. Valuations may reflect<br>expectations which turn out in hindsight to be incorrect, but the valuations<br>themselves are not irrational or inscrutable.</p>
<p>Issues to be Considered</p>
<p>1. Number and Type of Stocks Held</p>
<p>■ Even within a domestic equity mandate, we strongly believe that the number of<br>holdings should be increased in order to reduce stock-specific risk.</p>
<p>■ This will require an increase in the number of investment professionals available<br>to follow these companies - a change which we advocate.</p>
<p>■ We believe that the overall fund should have the ability to invest in non-<br>domestic equities as a matter of strategy.</p>
<p>■ However, international equities should be purchased as a strategic issue rather<br>than as a stock-specific issue. Purchasing the occasional foreign stock against a<br>domestic benchmark is simply obscuring the performance issue without adding<br>significantly to risk reduction.</p>
<p>Sixth Fund</p>
<p>Overall View</p>
<p>■ This seems to be one of the most professionally run group of the six funds.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>92</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärderine av första, andra, tredie, fiärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltnine 1993-1997</p>
<p>■ The Managing Director and other senior staff all have financial market<br>experience.</p>
<p>■ Equally important, the board is small and (we have been told) also has some<br>members who are investment professionals. These qualities are important for<br>flexibility and proper oversight.</p>
<p>• The Fund is making an effort to set up benchmarks which are in line with<br>generally accepted practice in the fund management industry. However there is<br>no generally accepted industry benchmark for venture Capital<sup>2</sup>.</p>
<p>■ The Fund is aware of the importance of risk control and is making an effort to<br>ensure risk monitoring to a high standard. Again, there is no industry standard<br>for venture Capital.</p>
<p>Problems to be Addressed</p>
<p>1. “Benchmark” for Venture Capital Activities</p>
<p>■ We believe that it will be necessary to establish long-term return guidelines<br>which are agreeable both to the Fund and to its sponsor. If no such guidelines<br>are established, there is a risk of disappointment in this highly volatile and<br>highly risky asset class for which there is no industry-accepted standard<br>benchmark type.</p>
<p>■ We believe that it is the Fund (rather than the govemment) which should<br>establish this benchmark in line with its own market studies and the supervision<br>of its Board of Directors. We understand that the Fund is attempting to deal<br>with this issue at the moment.</p>
<p>• Venture Capital is an area of activity which carries a high degree of risk.</p>
<p>■ For example, experience in the United States suggests that Intemal Rates of<br>Retum (IRR) in venture Capital investments over a ten year period are only very<br>modestly higher (e.g. less than 0.5%) than listed equities.</p>
<p>• US experience also suggests that a large proportion of investments are ultimately<br>unsuccessful over that time period.</p>
<p>2. Ability to Invest in “Overseas Equities”</p>
<p>■ We believe that the Fund should have the ability to continue to hold equity in<br>its former venture Capital investments which are subsequently purchased by<br>non-Swedish companies.</p>
<p>■ The Sixth Fund is the only Fund which is precluded from investing in “overseas<br>equities”; these have been defined as any company whose parent company is<br>not listed in Sweden.</p>
<p>• While the rationale for the existence of the Fund is clearly to nurture fledgling<br>Swedish companies, the fund should not be prevented from continuing to<br>benefit from successful companies which are purchased by foreign entities.</p>
<p>• Given the fact that risks are much higher at the very early stages of a company’s<br>existence, it does not make sense to prevent the initial provider of risk Capital<br>from benefiting from a more mature and successful company simply because<br>the company has been purchased by a foreign entity.</p>
<p>• Guidelines should be put in place, however, to prevent these holdings from<br>becoming large and structural over the very long term.</p>
<p><sup>2</sup> The term “venture Capital” is used here in its generic sense to encompass ail of the Sixth Fund’s<br>activities in non-listed equities. We do not make the distinction which the Fund does between<br>Private Equity and Venture Capital.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>
<p>93</p>
<p>98-05-26</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Finansdepartementet</h6>
<p>Utvärdering av första, andra, tredie, fjärde, femte och sjätte AP-fondens förvaltning 1993-1997</p>
<p>Issues to be Considered</p>
<p>1. Investments in Listed Securities</p>
<p>• The issue as to whether the Sixth Fund should be holding any listed securities at<br>all should be given some consideration, but this can only be considered as part<br>of the overall structure of all the Funds.</p>
<p>• We understand that each of the Funds (One through Six) has been given its<br>own “pot” of money to manage according to the guidelines it has been given.</p>
<p>• The Sixth fund is investing in listed securities until it is able to invest the<br>majority of its money in venture Capital.</p>
<p>■ Fully investing in venture Capital can take years (e.g. 5 to 8) and it is not<br>unusual for some investments to be redeemed before all the initial Capital is<br>invested.</p>
<p>■ For the above reason, it can be expected that a venture Capital operation will<br>always need access to a “draw down” source of funds.</p>
<p>■ As the Funds are currently structured, there is no alternative for the Sixth Fund<br>but to invest in listed securities in the manner in which it is doing now.</p>
<p>■ However, we do not believe that a team of two fund managers (one for equities<br>and one for fixed income) plus an assistant is “best practice” in terms of<br>managing listed assets over the long term.</p>
<p>■ We do see a role for the Sixth Fund over the longer term as a specialist investor<br>in venture Capital and private equity.</p>
<p>2. Sixth Fund as Venture Capital Specialist</p>
<p>■ Rather than being given its own “pot” of funds, an alternative is for the Sixth<br>Fund to play the role of venture Capital specialist in a more unified management<br>structure.</p>
<p>■ It should have easy access to an agreed amount of “draw down” funds for<br>venture Capital opportunities, but other “organisations” (either extemal or<br>intemal) would be specialists dedicated to managing surplus funds in asset<br>classes such as cash, fixed interest or equity markets.</p>
<p>3. Benchmark for Listed Swedish Equities</p>
<p>■ If the Sixth Fund continues to manage listed equities, we suggest the use of one<br>equity index to calculate both benchmark risk and performance retum.</p>
<p>■ The Sixth Fund uses the Affärsvärlden index to benchmark investment exposure<br>and the Findata index for calculating performance.</p>
<p>• The Findata index provides total retum figures which are necessary for<br>performance calculation, but the Affärsvärlden index provides only Capital retum<br>figures.</p>
<p>• Nonetheless, the Affärsvärlden index has been considered the industry standard<br>local benchmark index.</p>
<p>■ We suggest that perhaps the Findata index should be considered for both<br>purposes, now that the Findata index composition is similar to that of the<br>Affärsvärlden index.</p>
<p>■ An alternative is that perhaps the govemment could persuade the providers of<br>the Affärsvärlden index to calculate total retums.</p>
<p>■ This is a minor point, but the use of one index for both risk measurement &amp;<br>performance purposes is more transparent. We do not deem ourselves to be<br>experts on the Swedish equity market, however, and we defer to the expertise<br>of the Boards of the Funds.</p>
<p>William M. Mercer AB<br>Investment Consulting</p>

<p>7.2.1 Mercer View</p>
<p>7.2.1.1 Mandate, Restrictions &amp; Investment Approach</p>
<p>The First to Third Funds suffer from the same problem as all the other funds in<br>terms of having a legal benchmark which is too vague to serve as a guideline for<br>practice, or indeed, to measure performance in such a way tbat all parties can<br>easily determine whether or not performance is satisfactory. In the case of these<br>Funds, however, there is no obvious existing benchmark which is a satisfactory<br>measure of portfolio performance, given the buge market share the Funds<br>represent in tbe domesticfixed income markets.</p>
<p>There seems to be a need for an a benchmark index comprising a representative<br>proportion of outstanding domestic issues, botb public and private. Ideally, this<br>index would be constructed, maintained and valued by a non-govemmental third<br>party. Performance objectives against tbe benchmark cannot, in faimess to the<br>managers, look for a significant amount of value-added. The amount of value-<br>added would probably need to be the subject of furtber investigation. On tbe<br>other hand, there is no reason for active (as opposed to passive) management, if<br>tbe target is to acbieve index performance.</p>
<p>Establishing such a benchmark index would allow the Funds to set a duration<br>policy relative to tbat benchmark. It is understandable why the managers<br>currently wish to use an absolute duration target, but this does not make for tbe<br>clarity tbat is needed in order to assess performance accuratefy. A well-defined<br>index would satisfy the need for transparency of performance measurement.</p>
<p>The fund is allowed to invest up to 10% of total funds in non-domestic fixed<br>income securities. Managing a multi-country portfolio requires making a series<br>of projections regarding country retums, currency retums (including</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[95]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>First to Third AP Funds</p>
<p>assumptions for pairs of cross currencies) and interest rate directions. This is<br>an exercise in tbe management of an exponential amount of information. At tbe<br>most resourced end of tbe spectrum, “serious” global fixed income managers<br>may bave sixty people on the team and spend millions of dollars on information<br>tecbnology dedicated to global fixed income. Assuming tbat two investment<br>professional’s and four analysts can do tbis task adequately (even “just” tbe G5<br>index) requires a long leap of faitb. This is not a comment on tbe<br>professionalism of tbe individuals, simply a matter of wbere resource is best<br>expended. Outsourcing tbese funds may bave been the correct idea in tbe first<br>place.</p>
<p>Finally, within the domestic markets, it should be pointed out that the fixed<br>income funds, in addition to tbe equity funds, also benefit from an information<br>advantage. Tbe advantage is derived mainly from the Funds’ size, and size is a<br>two-edged sword.</p>
<p>1.2.1.2 People</p>
<p>Tbe most obvious comment for tbe First to Third Funds, as well as for all tbe<br>Funds except the Sixtb, is tbe question of the Board structure. In tbis case, tbe<br>problem of 14 plus 14 people is multiplied by three! Wbile the Investment<br>Committee acting as advisor to Lennart Nilsson is a pragmatic solution to keep<br>daily operations working, it still remains a fact tbat three board meetings are<br>required along with three sets of approvat There are two issues bere: the<br>capability of the board members and tbe prospect for lack of control.</p>
<p>All tbe Funds we interviewed (except tbe Sixtb) agreed tbat it is often tbe case<br>that tbey must explain tbe workings of tbe financial markets, and transactions<br>tbey intend to do, before the Boards an approve them. Tbis raises tbe obvious<br>question about whether or not the Boards are actually able to exercise due care<br>and control. It is possible that tbey may veto an action tbat would be beneficial to<br>tbe funds; it is equally possible tbat they may approve sometbing tbat tbey<br>would not otberwise approve witb a fuller understanding of tbe issues. Tbere is<br>also an issue that as tbere is only one “fund”, the three boards must agree to<br>make tbe same decisions (in tbeory, independently). Tbis is a recipe for lack of<br>accountability and/or concentration of power in the bands of tbe Chairman or<br>Managing Director.</p>
<p>At tbe very least, it seems that one board witb fewer people sbould be considered.<br>It sbould additionally be considered whether tbe board should not also consist<br>of people witb business management and financial manager experience;<br>presumably sucb individuals migbt be found even within tbe constituencies wbicb<br>need to be involved in the boards from a political point of view.</p>
<p>7.2.2 History of the First to Third Funds</p>
<p>The First through Third Funds were the first of the AP Funds to be set up in 1960. The<br>original motive behind the creation of these funds was to develop a pool of “private<br>saving” for the public pension scheme via direct deductions from the wages of the labour<br>force. All six Funds now act a buffer to the Swedish pay-as-you-go public pension<br>scheme but it is the First to Third Funds only which collect pension contributions on<br>behalf of the govemment as well as pay out gross pension payments. The First to Third</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[96]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>First to Third AP Funds</h6>
<p>Funds are also the source of cash inflow to the Fourth and Fifth Funds which are allowed<br>to draw from the First to Third Funds according to pre-set limits. The Fourth and Fifth<br>funds are also required to pay out 3% on the CPI-adjusted Capital to the First to Third<br>Funds based on the inflation-adjusted Capital value of the monies they originally drew.</p>
<p>Prior to the midd-eighties, there was a net inflow of contributions into the pension<br>system. In 1990, contributions were cash-flow neutral. In 1997, contributions were cash-<br>flow negative at SKr. 87.7 billion and pension payments SKr. 121.6 billion. In 1996, net<br>contributions were net negative with payments exceeding contributions by about SKr.<br>33-0 billion; however, Capital appreciation in 1996 contributed SKr. 36.8 billion in that<br>year. In 1997, Capital appreciation of SKr. 4.5 billion did not compensate for net cash<br>outflow of SKr. 33 9 billion.</p>
<p>7.2.3 The Mandate &amp; Restrictions</p>
<p>According to the English translation of the regulations goveming the AP Funds', the First<br>to Third Funds mandate is to invest: “...such that the long-term high yield<sup>!</sup>, satisfactory<br>liquidity and risk spreading requirements, together with those of satisfactory security, are<br>met.” The Funds may invest in: Swedish debt with “low credit risk”, debt of Swedish<br>limited companies or co-operative societies, up to 10% of the Funds in foreign debt and<br>up to 5% of the Funds in property. The fund board has decided to keep liquidity of up to<br>one year’s gross payments of the total govemment pension payments (e.g. SKr. 121.6<br>billion in 1997).</p>
<p>7.2.4 The First to Third Funds’ Intemal Guidelines &amp; Fund Structure</p>
<p>As their official Fund guidelines, the First to Third Funds have set the target of beating the<br>Swedish wage index and to achieve real returns such that the real value of assets is<br>maintained.</p>
<p>As of the end of 1997, total assets managed by the First to Third Funds amounted to SKr.</p>
<p>605.6 billion. Fixed Income investments accounted for 85.7% of the fund, CPI-linked<br>bonds for 10.1% and property for 4.7% of the total. At the end of 1997, the fixed income<br>portion<sup>3</sup> of the portfolio was comprised as follows:</p>

<p>property, but we were told that the Funds are becoming increasingly interested in the<br>REIT and listed property fund markets.</p>
<p>In practice, the Funds have tried to devise intemal benchmark indices based on the<br>Swedish fixed income markets, but this has proven difficult. We spoke with Elisabeth<br>Hansson, a fixed income analyst, who has spent a considerable amount of time working<br>on fixed income performance and benchmarks. Until 1996, the Fund used an intemally<br>devised and calculated benchmark index in which they wanted to take account of the<br>entire outstanding debt issuance in Sweden including govemment bonds, quasi-<br>govemment bonds (mortgage bonds, local authorities), corporates, etc.</p>
<p>More recently, the Funds have been using another internally-calculated benchmark index<br>for Swedish nominal fixed income securities which consists of 55% govemment issues,<br>35% mortgage bonds and 10% T-bills. For the index-linked securities, the Funds use the<br>OMRX Real index which consists of all five of the index-linked issues. For the<br>approximately 1.7% of the fund invested outside of Sweden, the fund uses the Salomon<br>Brothers’ G5 Index.</p>
<p>7.2.5 People</p>
<p>Because the First to Third Funds decided to co-operate in managing money from their<br>establishment in 1960, the asset management organisation must report to three separate<br>Boards each consisting of 14 delegates and 14 deputies who are appointed by the<br>govemment. Each board meets separately and each must approve the actions of the full-<br>time asset managers. In order to make this process a bit more efficient, an Investment<br>Committee has been established. The Investment Committee serves in the capacity of an<br>advisory board to Lennart Nilsson, the CEO of the fund management organisation and<br>consists of the three Chairmen of the board plus six board members (usually, but not<br>always, two people from each board). The Investment Committee meets seven times per<br>year versus five meetings per year for the boards. The Committee will usually try to meet<br>just before the board meetings in order to make recommendations for the Boards’<br>approval.</p>
<p>Since the Fund boards are charged with the mission of establishing the investment policy,<br>one annual meeting in February is devoted to establishing the annual investment strategy<br>for the coming year. (The funds call this their “strategic asset allocation” for the year, but<br>it is probably more accurately called a medium-term strategy.) The boards’ main goal in<br>setting “strategic asset allocation” is to protect Capital from the effects of inflation and to<br>give more specific guidance to the asset management organisation for the year. The<br>boards are also charged with setting guidance on all aspects of risk (including credit risk<br>and use of derivatives), on personnel policy and on the overall budget for the asset<br>management organisation.</p>
<p>Bo Ljunglöf, Deputy Managing Director, heads up the team which manages fixed income<br>securities.</p>
<p>7.2.6 Investment Process</p>
<p>It must be remembered that the First to Third Funds manage about 24% of the<br>govemment interest-bearing market (money market instruments plus fixed income<br>instruments). Having such a huge percentage of the market will have a significant effect<br>on how anyone manages money and, indeed, on how much value they are able to add to</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[98]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>First to Third AP Funds</h6>
<p>portfolios at the margin. Most of the added value is probably going to come from “sector<br>selection” (e.g. govemment vs. corporate bonds vs. mortgage bonds vs. index-linked).</p>
<p>Duration decisions are suggested to the board by a team of three people - Anders Palmer<br>and two fixed income analysts. The board then ratifies this decision. Previously, the<br>Funds made the duration decision relative to the benchmark. The strategic decision is set<br>annually, but the investment horizon is two to three years. It appears that absolute<br>duration is being set relative to a qualitative impression of the average duration of all<br>outstanding Swedish issues.</p>
<p>(Sekretessbelagd information). We asked whether the team attempt to take positions such<br>as “bullets” (concentrating the holdings in one duration segment) or “barbells” (holding<br>only long and short term paper for a total portfolio duration close to index). Because of<br>the size of the Funds, these sorts of positions cannot be implemented in the way a typical<br>fund manager might, but the team does from time to time over- and under-weight<br>different duration sectors.</p>
<p>We asked if the Funds suffered any liquidity problems in the course of trying to trade the<br>portfolio and Ljunglöf reckoned that there were not. He stated that moving from a<br>duration of, say, 2.30 to 2.32, is not a problem. Of course, with such a short portfolio,<br>issues will be constantly redeemed which gives an opportunity for the tactical position to<br>evolve over time.</p>
<p>We asked about the overseas bonds which represent about 1.7% of the total portfolio.<br>Lunglöf stated that the Funds are “not that active” overseas. The internal benchmark is<br>the Salomon Brothers G5 index. Up until about a year and a half ago, all the overseas<br>bonds were extemally managed, so the G5 index was chosen as something of a “safe”<br>investment choice. There is one external manager also managing against this index.<br>Lunglöf stated that the decision between country and currency is analysed and taken<br>separately. The portfolios are 100% hedged back into Swedish Krone. Value-added will<br>come mainly from country (and or currency?) selection and secondly from duration. The<br>managers will cross-hedge between G5 currencies.</p>
<p>We asked about use of derivatives and Lunglöf stated that they used to use futures a lot,<br>but they don’t any more. This is mainly because they take delivery of the physical bonds<br>when the futures expire and they have to take whichever issues are in the futures contract<br>- the fund wants to pick and choose the instruments it invests in.</p>
<p>(Sekretessbelagd information)</p>
<p>7.2.7 Risk Control</p>
<p>We spoke with Carl Nyreröd, an analyst in the Risk Control department (the head of the<br>department, Mikael Gruditz, was home with the ‘flu). Carl outlined that the department is<br>responsible for control of six kinds of risk: credit risk, interest rate risk, foreign exchange<br>risk, derivative risk, legal risk and liquidity risk. In some organisations, these risk control<br>functions might be the responsibility of the fixed income team (e.g. credit risk).</p>
<p>Credit risk, (sekretessbelagd information)</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[991</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>First to Third AP Funds</h6>
<p>Interest rate risk. The monitoring of interest rate risk has been “passive” up to now, but<br>the Funds are hoping to monitor interest rate risk more actively when the new IT systems<br>are installed. Today the team mainly checks to make certain that the duration of the<br>portfolios are in line with Board policy and that the intemal benchmark is calculated<br>correctly. Foreign exchange risk. Again, the monitoring is fairly passive rather than pro-<br>active. The Risk Control team monitors that the Boards’ guidelines are not being violated<br>(they can take active currency positions between the G5 currencies).</p>
<p>Derivative risk. The Risk Control team took an active part in setting out intemal<br>guidelines for the use of derivatives and this guidance was then ratified by the Boards.<br>Guidelines have set out what kinds of derivatives may be used and for what purpose,<br>counterparties, amounts and the kinds of legal agreements (if any) which need to be in<br>place. The a decision from the fund board State that:</p>
<p>■ An policy must exist;</p>
<p>• No options are allowed;</p>
<p>■ The derivatives policy must comply with other risk policies;</p>
<p>* The derivatives policy must comply with the regulations of the Swedish<br>Financial Supervisory Board;</p>
<p>■ Intemal instructions must be established.</p>
<p>The intemal instructions State that:</p>
<p>■ The aim of the instructions is best practice;</p>
<p>■ Instructions must be in accordance with existing laws;</p>
<p>■ The asset managers will mainly use standard derivative contracts;</p>
<p>■ The derivatives instructions must comply with other risk policies (various<br>limitations for different exposures are listed);</p>
<p>■ The asset managers must have sufficient Software tools, knowledge of derivative<br>instruments and intemal control.</p>
<p>Legal risks covers mainly the legal contracts within the private placements market and<br>sometimes within derivative contracts. The main point about liquidity risk is it is the Risk<br>Control unit’s duty to make certain that one year’s liquidity is maintained at all times.</p>
<p>Performance Measurement</p>
<p>Our last visit was with Göran Styrman, Financial Manager and Elisabeth Hansson, Analyst<br>Fixed Income. Styrman heads up the performance team of five people, but this is not a<br>full-time job for any of the participants. The performance team is chaired by him and<br>consists of Hansson, Mikael Grunditz, the controller of the property company and the<br>Administrative Manager (Styrman’s direct superior).</p>
<p>Much of the information from this meeting has already been covered elsewhere in this<br>note as Hansson is mainly responsible for calculating the intemal benchmark index. This<br>team is also looking forward to the new IT system which should allow them to report<br>performance on all asset classes on a weekly basis. Presently, their system does not allow<br>them to measure performance more frequently than once a month in index-linked<br>securities, derivatives and foreign investments. Styrman told us that the Funds use the<br>IPD property index as the benchmark for their property investments in Britain and the<br>Wilshire REIT index for their REIT investments. They will be using a index for Swedish<br>property which is now being compiled by a Swedish property firm and should be ready</p>

<p>for 1999 - they feel that there is good similarity between the kinds of domestic property<br>they own and that of the new index.</p>
<p>The performance team expressed their internal benchmarks for the following asset classes:</p>
<p>■ Fixed income. On a five year basis, return at least the same as the intemal<br>benchmark index.</p>

<p>7.3.1 Mercer View</p>
<p>7.3.1.1 Mandate, Restrictions &amp; Investment Approach</p>
<p>The Fourth Fund suffers from the same problem as all tbe other funds in terms of<br>having a legal benchmark which is too vague to serve as a guideline for practice,<br>or indeed, to measure performance in such a way that all parties can easily<br>determine wbetber or not performance is satisfactory. The Fortb Fund to bas<br>adapted to tbe vagueness of the legislation by pursuing a strategy of investing in<br>a relatively small number of stocks in companies considered to bave long-term<br>growth.</p>
<p>Managing nearly SKr. 100 billion of assets with a handful of people is an unusual<br>and interesting problem. The Fourth Fund’s approach bas essentially been to use<br>their information advantage given their status at the centre of the close-knit<br>Swedish financial community. They recognise the critical need to get the few big<br>holdings rigbt, even to the extent of active shareholder participation in<br>management cbange in tbe example of Volvo. Given that 50% of tbe fund is tbe<br>five largest holdings, most of the rest of what they do is very mucb of second<br>order importance. Tbe approach to overseas investment seems particularly<br>naive although they did not really claim otherwise - likewise they did not claim<br>any magic in valuing sbares, or even identifying growth - more of a preparation<br>to take the long view and ignore fluctuations over the short-term. Tbey do not<br>appear to like selling shares, unless they feel the outlook has deteriorated<br>significantly, but mucb prefer to buy and bold.</p>
<p>7.3.1.2 People</p>
<p>Tbe most obvious comment for the Fourth Fund, as well as for all the Funds but<br>the Sixth, is tbe Board size of 14 members plus 14 deputies. This unwieldy<br>number should be seriously reconsidered.. The small working group wbicb the<br>Fourth Fund uses to approve transactions belps day-to-day activity significantly;<br>tbe otherfunds all bave parallel small decision-making groups.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[102]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Fourth AP Fund</p>
<p>History &amp; Background</p>
<p>The original AP Funds system was created back in 1960 when equities were not a<br>consideration for the govemment. Late in thel960s, early 1970s, equities were debated<br>and the Fourth Fund was established in 1974. At the time the debate was about whether<br>govemment funds should “control” equities and thereby control companies, or whether<br>they should just aim for high return. In practice all four of the Managing Directors since<br>1974 have aimed for high return (rather than anything more “political”).</p>
<p>7.3.2 Mandate &amp; Restrictions</p>
<p>The long-term goal is regarded as “long term return over five year periods somewhat<br>above the Stockholm market index including dividends”. According to “the regulations”<br>paragraph 35, the Fund’s assets shall be invested such that the long-term high yield,<br>satisfactory liquidity and risk-spreading requirements are met. The Fund is restricted to a<br>maximum of 10% of total funds in overseas equities and a maximum of 10% of the issued<br>share Capital of any individual quoted company. They are also not allowed to consolidate<br>(ie own more than 50% of an unquoted company).</p>
<p>The Fund can draw on 1% of the First to Third Funds’ value (in order to increase Capital)<br>but this has not been done recently. Total Capital invested at original book cost is SKr. 5<br>billion; indexed to inflation this runs to SKr. 10 billion against a current market value of<br>SKr. 96.5 billion - hence the additional SKr. 1 billion that could be drawn is seen as<br>unimportant. A retum of 3% in the indexed Capital value is repaid to the First to Third<br>Funds.</p>
<p>Total retum has until recently been measured against the VA index adjusted (intemally)<br>for dividends. Since February 1998 the benchmark index has changed to the Findata<br>Index which is a total return index.</p>
<p>Legally regarded as a govemment “authority,” much of information produced by the Fund<br>is in the public domain, which restricts what can be written down and kept confidential<br>unless a counterparty is involved.</p>
<p>7.3.3 Relationship with the Board</p>
<p>The Board of Directors is 28 strong, nominated by various organisations (as set out in the<br>regulation), with the Chairman and Vice-Chairman appointed by the govemment. They<br>regard the board as more supervisory than executive but that it is viewed as a “well of a<br>strength” when it comes to some issues (e.g. corporate governance).</p>
<p>Within the formal restrictions, the Board has determined a policy of concentration in a<br>relatively small number of holdings, despite the fund representing 3 4% of the whole<br>Swedish Market.</p>
<p>There are quarterly meetings with the Board (copy of most recent presentations thereto is<br>on file) with the aim of discussions being to outline the direction the fund is looking to<br>move in (and avoiding either detail of individual stocks, or surprises). In practical terms<br>the discussion with the Board focuses on three levels:</p>
<p>1. Liquidity - why has the fund the level of liquidity that it has (normal range 5-10%)?</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[103]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Fourth AP Fund</h6>
<p>2. &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Sectors - how different is the fund from its neutral weighting of 3.4% across the<br>whole Swedish market? Why is the fund over- and under-weight the various<br>sectors?</p>
<p>3. &nbsp;&nbsp;Stocks - all positions in Companies of Value SKr. 7.5bn or more are looked at<br>relative to benchmark weighting, and recognising that the real focus needs to be on<br>the few largest holdings which will have the biggest impact on the performance of<br>the portfolio.</p>
<p>Outside the Board Meetings TH occasionally talks to the Chairman (and/or Vice<br>Chairman, other members of the Working Committee) if large or sensitive issues arise.<br>This Committee does not Schedule meetings formally but meets a couple of times a year.</p>
<p>7.3.4 Overseas Equities</p>
<p>The limit on foreign equities was increased to 10% in 1989 although less than 2% is<br>actually invested in overseas shares. The aim behind these holdings, which are in the six<br>major markets, has been to “build bridges” with the world outside Sweden (especially the<br>London financial community) in order to increase knowledge and get a better<br>understanding of International markets.</p>
<p>The issue has changed somewhat - the major international brokers are coming to Sweden<br>and they are keen to invest with the Fourth Fund given its assets, so there is no problem<br>of access. The current stock-picking approach in the major markets, in order to do<br>business with the big international brokers may be becoming redundant.</p>
<p>The issue of a philosophy for the foreign portfolio has been raised initially with the<br>Board, but conclusions have not yet been reached. TH said he had explained a global<br>asset allocation model to the Board, but one of them said it looked like a hailstorm.</p>
<p>The second issue is that of Swedish companies merging and being taken over and losing<br>their domestic status. This accounts, in part, for the low current overseas weighting as<br>more of the 10% could be used up if one of the big holdings “goes international”. It also<br>raises the question of whether a pan- European sector approach could be eventually be<br>more appropriate than the current 90% Sweden 10% the rest.</p>
<p>7.3.5 Investment Approach</p>
<p>TH described the investment approach as top down in terms of the sector choice coming<br>first, but interactive with the bottom up, especially in terms of valuation. At the start of<br>each year, a macro-economic scenario is agreed upon for the major economies and<br>variables (copy on file) updated in mid-year if necessary. This is to give the analysts<br>consistent conditions for their profit forecasts. It remains focused on the long-term which<br>means shares are “bought to be owned, not to be sold”, and therefore tumover is low (10-<br>15% pa). In practice TH admitted that their success had been more in picking the right<br>stocks within sectors, whereas the record of picking sectors was rather mixed.</p>
<p>The portfolio is concentrated, owning around 53 of Swedish stocks of the 350 or so in the<br>market and of about 200 which could be invested in. Within that 53, the top 18 holdings<br>account for 87% of the domestic equity portfolio, of which 17 are either in-line or<br>overweight holdings. The top five holdings are 50% of this total domestic portfolio!</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[104]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Fourth AP Fund</h6>
<p>As a large institution they have good relations with the banks and stock brokers, so<br>analysts come and tell them their stories. The in-house analysts then seek to do the little<br>bit of extra analysis, including visiting companies. The bottom line of this analysis is<br>profit projections for the next three years plus an indication of a guideline for valuation.<br>However, growth is much more focused on than valuation and TH argued that their<br>valuation methodology was no different from anyone else’s, they do have an intemal list<br>of stocks with “guidelines” (target?) prices - which we were able to see existed but were<br>not able to look at! Valuation yardsticks include price/eamings ratio and P/E relatives,<br>although international companies are increasingly being valued more against to price /<br>cash-flow measures.</p>
<p>As well as information from brokers analysts they are also prepared to buy in additional<br>expertise as appropriate - e.g. macro-economics analysis, legal advice etc. In practical<br>terms, the small nature of the Swedish financial community and the size of the Fourth<br>fund’s assets under management (prestige as TH kept mentioning) plus its known long-<br>term bias, given the team high levels of access to the management of Swedish companies<br>which outsiders would find it difficult or irresponsible to match.The fourth fund is having<br>better information by virtue of their contacts, experience and position as AP Funds.</p>
<p>What we are saying is that their advantage is in having good access to management,<br>information flows etc, not that they are obtaining information the should not have.</p>
<p>In analysing companies TH stated the focus was on growth, but also the stability of that<br>growth, with the analytical framework being the three “Ms”.</p>
<p>Management - how good are they</p>
<p>- what are their ideas (strategy for the business)</p>
<p>- what is the ownership structure</p>
<p>Markets - what is the Company’s market position and growth</p>
<p>opportunity (real growth ie above inflation)</p>
<p>Money - how is company financed</p>
<p>- &nbsp;what is the quality of profit</p>
<p>Of these, management and markets are regarded as most important; understanding<br>management comes first, whereas a changing view about a company’s market can lead to<br>a decision to sell the shares. The approach is much less focused on profit forecasts for<br>this year and next, with the investment tearris knowledge of management enabling them<br>to sift through the excess of information supplied (by brokers).</p>
<p>7.3.6 Dealing &amp; Options</p>
<p>All the dealing is done by Anne Loow and the settlement is done separately by the back<br>office. Stocks are held by Nordbanken (Sweden) and State Street in Munich for overseas.<br>Derivatives are used very infrequently, and not at all in the last 18 months. Auditors have<br>made a special report on intemal Controls (copy supplied). Back office procedures<br>include monthly reconciliations.</p>

<p>Fifth AP Fund</p>

<p>7.4.1 Mercer View</p>
<p>7.4.1.1 Mandate, Restrictions &amp; Investment Approach</p>
<p>The Fifth Fund suffers from tbe same problem as all tbe other funds in terms of<br>having a legal benchmark which is too vague to serve as a guideline for practice,<br>or indeed, to measure performance in such a way tbat all parties can easily<br>determine wbetber or not performance is satisfactory. Tbe Fifth Fund to bas<br>adapted to the vagueness of the legislation by pursuing a strategy of investing in<br>a small number of stocks in companies considered to bave long-term growth.</p>
<p>Managing SKr. 15 billion witb three investment professionals, the restriction of<br>10% overseas, and a Board guideline of 20 or so holdings, implies an approach<br>different from tbe average fund management house. Tbe basic issue here is<br>having an insufficient number of investment professionals to bave good coverage<br>of tbe Swedish market; tbis is offset by an information advantage wbicb tbe<br>Fund enjoys as a result of tbe contacts of tbe Managing Director and simply by<br>virtue of being tbe govemment pension fund.</p>
<p>Tbe team’s approach is to pick bigb growth stocks/sectors (no magic bere) and<br>back them for tbe long term — wbilst avoiding low growth industrial/cyclicals.<br>Clearly, tbe investment process focuses almost exclusively on growth and bardly<br>at all on price (altbougb using options to extract small value from underfying<br>large holdings redresses that sligbtly). Tbis approach might bear fruit over time<br>(in terms of good results) but it depends on tbe market systematicaUy<br>undervaluing long-term growth stocks rather tban tbe team at tbe Fifth Fund<br>identifying growth that tbe market has not noticed In tbis context, it is difficult<br>to assess wbetber or not tbe Fund is able to use its information advantage to add<br>value to tbe portfolio.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[106]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Fifth AP Fund</p>
<p>The Fifth fund is limited to investing up to 10% of its funds in foreign assets, in<br>common with all tbe other Funds except the Sixtb which may not own any foreign<br>assets at all We suggest tbat sbares listed in the Swedish Affärsvärlden index<br>sbould not be counted as foreign holdings. In addition, foreign holdings sbould<br>probably be the subject of an overall strategy review incorporating all six Funds.<br>Tbe Fifth (and Fourth) fund are furtber limited to holding 10% of the share<br>Capital of an individual company. Again, this too should probably be the subject<br>of an overall strategy review; at SKr. 15 billion, this limit is only a problem if tbe<br>Fund is managing small and medium-sized companies.</p>
<p>The Fifth fund has adopted the VA equity index as its benchmark and tbey<br>measure performance against the Findata index. 4s tbe Findata index is a total<br>retum index and total retum is needed to measure performance, it would be<br>neater to use tbe Findata index for both purposes. Given the long-term growth<br>orientation to the portfolio approach, however, it appears tbat the “actual”<br>benchmark for the Fund is its requirement to pay out 3% plus inflation. The Fund<br>has given itself a 10% cash limit which tbe Board allegedly regards as too bigb.<br>We would regard levels of 3% to 5% casb as transactional and a 10% maximum as<br>being usual practice for most fund managers who consider themselves to be fully<br>invested. Wbile tbe govemment may wish to review the 10% figure, we do not<br>regard it as unduly bigb or unusuaL</p>
<p>7.4.1.2 People</p>
<p>The most obvious comment for the Fifth Fund, as well as for all the Funds but tbe<br>Sixtb, is tbe Board size of 14 members plus 14 deputies wbicb tend to result in<br>Board meetings of 20+ people. Tbis unwieldy number should be seriously<br>reconsidered — along witb tbe possibility of one overall Board encompassing all<br>the funds. Tbe small subcommittee wbicb tbe Fifth Fund uses to approve<br>transactions probably helps day-to-day activity significantly; the other funds all<br>bave parallel small decision-making groups.</p>
<p>Tbe other comment is about tbe small size of tbe team. Three investment<br>professionals running SKr. 15 billion is small by any standards — regardless of<br>tbe team’s professionalism. The requirement to keep tbe number of stocks low<br>belps the Fund to keep abreast of company information but it also increases risk<br>against tbe benchmark, absolute risk (increased volatility from fewer<br>boldingsjand liquidity risk. Looking at tbe six funds as a wbole, does it really<br>make sense to bave only three people managing SKr. 15 billion in domestic<br>equities?</p>
<p>7A.2 History</p>
<p>In 1960 Funds 1-3 were established, originally as what he believes to be a political<br>“gimmick” rather than part of the national insurance system. No one is actually insured<br>with benefits tied to what the funds achieve; the original concept was related to ensuring<br>an adequate level of savings in the economy. The Fourth Fund was established in 1974,<br>the Fifth in 1989 and the Sixth in 1997. The original purpose of the equity funds<br>(beginning with 4) was to provide a source of risk Capital to Swedish companies at a time<br>when Capital markets were making this difficult. The Sixth fund was established to focus<br>on medium and smaller-sized companies.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>1107]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Fmn AP Fund</p>
<p>7.4.3 Mandate and Restrictions</p>
<p>LL regarded the fund as having one objective “good performance” and one restriction “not<br>to take too much risk”. In practical terms the fund is compared with Findatas index at the<br>total portfolio level (Swedish equities, overseas equities and cash) and the VAs index for<br>comparison of equity return. In terms of risk, with most of the fund in large cap Swedish<br>companies. LL believes that the risk against the benchmark index is seen as very low.</p>
<p>An additional formal restriction imposed by legislation is that the fund is not allowed to<br>own more than 10% of the issued share Capital of a company (which LL would like to see<br>resolved as it prevented them from obtaining meaningful holdings in smaller and<br>medium-sized companies). The Board has also made clear its view that the managers of<br>the fund are expected to know the stocks they own very well, and has indicated 20<br>holdings as a guideline to bear this out, although in practice the number of stocks owned<br>is a little higher. The Fund currently owns about 25 stocks; this calculations includes a<br>fund of ten small and medium-sized healthcare companies which are counted as one<br>holding.</p>
<p>LL’s view is that the basis for the existence of the Fourth and Fifth funds is investing in<br>equities with a long term perspective. Short-term trading is precluded both<br>philosophically (not believed to add value, but see options below) and practically,<br>because of the size of the funds relative in the Swedish market. Tumover is therefore<br>very low at 10-15% per annum.</p>
<p>7.4.4 Overseas Investment</p>
<p>The fund is now allowed to invest up to 10% in overseas equities. Whereas the Fourth<br>Fund has embraced overseas equities “more enthusiastically”, the Fifth Fund takes the<br>view that the logic for buying overseas equities was to improve performance.<br>Consequently the approach has been constrained to buying overseas companies which<br>are better than the Swedish counterparts, in desirable sectors — e.g. Roche instead of Astra<br>in the high growth pharmaceutical sector. The overseas investments are therefore only a<br>handful in number. Currency is not hedged.</p>
<p>7.4.5 Investment Approach</p>
<p>LL’s interpretation of the aim of investing in equities with a long-range perspective is to<br>build up exposure to high growth sectors or industries and have low exposure to<br>sectors/industries which are low/no growth. In practical terms the sectors identified as<br>having high growth prospects are pharmaceuticals, telecommunications, biotechnology<br>and information technology. Since his arrival in 1996, the Fund’s exposure to low growth<br>areas has been reduced by selling/scaling back holdings in Sydkraft (electricity), SKF<br>(bearings) and Volvo, for example whilst Netcom (mobile phone operator) has been a<br>major addition. These changes have been effected only gradually, partly because of<br>liquidity considerations and partly because the strategy remains essentially long term.</p>
<p>ACH gave a further insight into the investment approach by explaining how they had<br>identified biotechnology as a growth area, but recognising the riskiness of the individual<br>companies had sought provision from the board to allocate one “unit” into 10 holdings in<br>this area. They have an advisory panel of three professors to help them understand the<br>quality of the people and the research efforts in the companies, and so far have acquired<br>four holdings in the sub-sector. Value is not a major driver in the decision to buy - the</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>(1081</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Fifth AP Fund</h6>
<p>range of possible values for the type of company is very great - and stock is accumulated<br>over a period of time, starting with a small amount.</p>
<p>For large companies there is a lot of informal contact at management level, given LL’s<br>background and, the smallness of the community in Sweden. They also buy in research<br>from the leading Swedish brokers as well as favourite brokers based in London. Formal<br>meetings with companies tend to be few although they have recently made efforts to<br>meet with the foreign competitors of interesting companies to be able to understand<br>better what is going on in the industry (e.g. Air Liquide, Linde as competitors in the<br>industrial gas business to AGA).</p>
<p>The investment approach is clearly very growth orientated. LL said they didn’t bother<br>about the share price too much on a 1-2 year time horizon. They accept that their choice<br>of growth sectors has nothing proprietary about it and that Identification of growth areas<br>is an art, not a Science. What they do emphasise is picking companies that are growing<br>faster than GDP (or in markets growing faster) and avoiding those that are doing poorly.<br>They look at measures such as return on equity, retum on Capital employed and<br>operating margins (more to avoid losers than pick winners), and having identified growth<br>companies. watch them closely to ensure that nothing is changing to affect that growth.</p>
<p>As a small group (of 3) there is no need to have any formal meetings/structures. Ideas<br>are discussed among themselves. There is also a sub-committee of the board, comprising<br>the Chairman, Vice-Chairman and one other (total board is 28) which they meet with<br>every 5 or 6 weeks, and discuss changes in holdings, especially new stocks introduced<br>into the portfolio.</p>
<p>7.4.6 Options &amp; Dealing</p>
<p>They use options as a means to add a little bit of value at the margin, given the large and<br>illiquid nature of some of their holdings (e.g. selling covered calls when their long-term<br>favourite stocks look expensive). At any one time, exposure is only likely to be 1-2% of<br>the total portfolio, which apparently added 0.2% to the overall retum in 1997.</p>
<p>Dealing is done by EU, with Ann Marie Werin responsible for actually settling the<br>transactions (a sort of Chinese wall). LL signs off on all new holdings; Options can be<br>done by EU or ACH.</p>
<p>7.4.7 Other Points</p>
<p>They have around 10% on deposit as a cash holding at the moment, at the high end of<br>what they see as a normal range of 5-10% (the Board allegedly believes a 10% cash<br>holding is too high). They have been (incorrectly) cautious of the market.</p>
<p>The Board meets 4 times a year with the Sub-Committee more frequently. Reporting of<br>portfolio performance, transactions etc is done to the Sub-Committee. Public reports are<br>produced as at end April, end October and the annual one for end December.</p>
<p>IT has been installed using an outside consultant who has been working with the Fund<br>since 1989. The IT is regarded as adequate, but likely to have to be improved.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[1091</p>

<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Fifth AP Fund</h6>
<p>The fund is still receiving cash inflow (surplus return from Funds 1-3) but is also paying a<br>real rate of return of 3% on its original Capital (inflation adjusted). The net inflow in 1997<br>was SKr. 316 million.</p>
<p>LL concluded by saying that he was happy with the existing strategy and approach, and<br>did not worry too much about comparisons with the index. Over the longer-term he<br>expected that the 10% limit on overseas equity holdings would be raised (to perhaps 30%)<br>which would require a different approach from the current one. Hiring people in Sweden<br>may not necessarily be the right answer to gaining an expertise in non-Swedish equities.<br>The same problem might occur as pan-European industry consolidation leaves Sweden<br>with fewer and fewer investable companies.</p>

<p>7.5.1.1 Mandate &amp; Restrictions</p>
<p>Tbe Sixtb Fund suffers from the same problem as all tbe otber funds in terms of<br>b av ing a legal benchmark wbicb is too vague to serve as a guideline for practice,<br>or indeed, to measure performance in sucb a way tbat all parties can easify<br>determine wbetber or not performance is satisfactory. The Sixtb Fund seems to<br>bave adapted to tbe vagueness of tbe legislation a bit better than the otber funds,<br>all of wbom expressed a wisb to see clearer guidelines adopted witbin tbe<br>legislation.</p>
<p>Tbe Sixtb Fund may not invest in any securities outside of Sweden. Tbis<br>restriction includes companies listed in the Swedish Affärsvärlden equity index<br>and it also includes companies that tbe Fund may bave sponsored who get taken<br>over by a foreign parent. In the first case, tbe Fund is exposed to index risk in<br>tbat it cannot own some of tbe companies in tbe index sucb as Pharmacia<br>Vpjobn, Nokia and Autoliv. It would make more sense to allow all tbe Funds to<br>invest in all tbe companies in tbe domestic equity index and not define tbese<br>holdings as “foreign” at att. In the second incidence, tbe primary purpose of tbe<br>Sixtb Fund, to nurture fledgling Swedish companies, is not exactly tbwarted in<br>tbe event of a foreign take-over of tbat company, but sucb a purcbase would<br>preclude tbe Fund from enjoying the fruits of its investment. There should be an<br>acceptable level of total fund assets wbicb can be devoted to “foreign” assets on<br>tbe Private Equity side.</p>
<p>Tbe benchmarks set for Swedish bond and equity investments are logical and in<br>line witb current commercial practice. However, we note tbat tbe fund uses one</p>

<p>7.5.1.2 People</p>
<p>Witb five board members, tbe Sixtb Fund does not suffer from baving an<br>unwieldy number of people on tbe board.
