It might<br>be more logical to consider tbe Sixtb Fund as being a dedicated specialist in<br>Swedisb Private Equity development and to employ otber specialists (otber<br>specialist AP Funds or extemally) to manage listed securities. This is not a<br>condemnation of tbe individual managers in tbe Sixtb Fund - it is simply a<br>question of Fund structure and optimal use of resources.</p>
<p>7.5.2 History of the Sixth Fund</p>
<p>The Sixth AP Fund is the youngest of the AP Funds, having received its mandate from<br>Parliament in April 1996. The membership of the Fund’s board was decided by the<br>govemment and the Board officially constituted in July 1996. President Peter Sandberg<br>arrived at the Sixth fund in November 1996, having previously been CFO of AB Volvo ;<br>Head of Strategy- and Business Development, Fredrik Karlsson, also previously worked<br>with Sandberg at Volvo. In December 1996, the Sixth fund received SKr. 10 billion as a<br>one-time transfer from the First through Third Funds (mostly in T-Bills).</p>
<p>Initially, these monies were invested in fixed income and equity investments which still<br>remain the bulk of the fund. The intention is to carry on investing in quoted fixed<br>income and equity markets in order to grow the Capital available for private Capital<br>activities.</p>
<p>7.5.3 The Mandate &amp; Restrictions</p>
<p>The Sixth Fund sees as it primary “mission” to provide private equity Capital to develop<br>businesses in Sweden. Their actual mandate, according to The National Swedish Pension</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[112]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Sixth AP Fund</h6>
<p>Fund Regulations, is to “...be invested such that the long-term high yield, satisfactory<br>liquidity and risk spreading requirements are met.”<sup>1</sup></p>
<p>The Sixth Fund may invest in: shares in Swedish limited companies; in convertible debt<br>or other debt instruments linked to equity options in Swedish limited companies; in<br>private equity in Swedish co-operative societies; and in participation rights in Swedish<br>limited partnerships.</p>
<p>Note that, unlike the other AP Funds, the Sixth Fund is not allowed to invest in any non-<br>Swedish company, nor is it allowed to invest in any Swedish subsidiary of a company<br>whose parent is not Swedish. The Sixth Fund may hold up to 30% of the voting shares of<br>a company, although this regulation does not apply to Private Equity.</p>
<p>The final difference between the Sixth Fund and the other funds is that the Sixth Fund has<br>five Board members whereas all the others have 14 Board members plus 14 deputies.<br>Sandberg claimed that the members of the Sixth Fund’s board are all experienced in<br>financial markets.</p>
<p>7.5.4 The Sixth Fund’s Intemal Guidelines</p>
<p>In addition to the fairly broadly-worded mandate in the Regulations, each fund is<br>expected to adopt its own set of guidelines, including restrictions and benchmarks. The<br>Sixth Fund has adopted the Affärsvärlden index as the benchmark index for equities, (and<br>the Findata index for performance calculations); the OMRX index has been chosen for<br>fixed income. The private equity operations have no fixed benchmark, but the Fund are<br>working on ways of trying to incorporate appropriate risk/return measures into their<br>process for this asset class. Ideally, they would like to be able to measure risk/retum for<br>each component part of the Fund as well as for the entire fund.</p>
<p>As President of the Fund, Sandberg set out his priorities for developing the new<br>organisation. He articulates these priorities as:</p>
<p>1. Make certain that asset management is being conducted in a prudent and risk-aware<br>way.</p>
<p>2. Develop a good administrative capability;</p>
<p>3. Develop a rigorous and effective investment process;</p>
<p>4. Create a corporate culture and extemal profile/market niche for the Fund;</p>
<p>5. Carry out private equity investment in companies.</p>
<p>Point three is one that Sandberg came back to again and again during our meeting. He is<br>determined that investment process in all the different areas should be well-considered by<br>the team and set out in a documented manner. He is aware of the fact that process<br>should not be too rigid or performed in too rote a manner, but he feels that it is important<br>that process is carried out in a uniform way across the organisation. On the Private<br>Equity side, the Fund admits explicitly to being in a learning phase and to being in a<br>process of developing their own investment process. Every Private Equity deal that was<br>done during 1997 was documented step-by-step on a data management system and deals<br>were analysed after they were done. The outline of a process has been developed, but a<br>more specific articulation of the investment process will be taken further during 1998.</p>

<p>7.5.5 People</p>
<p>A list of the current team is shown in Appendix A according to their function within the<br>Fund. Appendix B gives a short précis of the team’s education, years investment<br>experience and their most immediate previous employer. As stated earlier, Peter<br>Sandberg and his deputy, Fredrik Karlsson as well as fixed income manager Fredrik Magni<br>(and Sandberg’s secretary!) came from Volvo.</p>
<p>Sandberg was keen to assemble a group of experienced people who wanted a new<br>challenge and who wanted to live in Göteborg - which he reckoned was a good selling<br>point. An advertisement was placed in the premier Swedish financial newspaper and got<br>about 2000 responses which Sandberg reckons were of high quality. He stated that he<br>wanted people with an entrepreneurial spirit who were business-minded, have high<br>personal integrity and who are motivated to achieve success. He wants a flat organisation<br>where everyone takes responsibility and works in a team without prima donnas. He was<br>keen to get people with a range of skills and experience.</p>
<p>Sandberg noted that he is still actively looking for someone to head up the Small and<br>Medium sized Enterprises effort as well as two more team members for the Venture<br>Capital Team.</p>
<p>7.5.6 Private Equity</p>
<p>The Private Equity activities are divided into three sub-categories of activity: Mergers and<br>Acquisitions (deals over SKr. 20 million), Small &amp; Medium Sized Enterprises (deals under<br>SKr. 20 million) and Venture Capital (currently restricted to the Life Sciences and<br>Technology industries).</p>
<p>The Fund is quite frank about the fact that these activities are still being built up. This has<br>several implications. First, the Fund does not yet feel that it has the entire Private Equity<br>team in place. Secondly, they realise that investing in this asset class will be a leaming<br>experience for the organisation in its entirety as well as for the particular individuals.<br>Sandberg has tried to compile a team which includes industry experts (e.g. in Life<br>Sciences and Technology) as well as investors and investment bankers but he and the<br>team are aware that there are not a lot of people in Sweden with direct experience in<br>investing in Private Equity. The goal, therefore, is to make certain that the Fund has and<br>builds a culture of being a “leaming organisation”.</p>
<p>As a leaming organisation, one strategy is to invest as an active partner in funds in<br>specialist areas such as Information Technology or simply in generalist private equity<br>funds. At the moment, the Fund hold approximately 10 funds, all of whom are managed<br>by organisations they know well. By acting in partnership with other investment<br>organisations, the Fund hopes to: a) build competence, b) diversify its Private Equity<br>investments, c) obtain an extemal viewpoint/analysis, d) increase the amount of funds<br>under management (they still only have limited direct Private Equity investments) and e)<br>build deal flow for the future.</p>
<p>Investment Process</p>
<p>There are four main stages of the Private Equity investment process: Deal Flow, Analysis,<br>Ownership Process, Exit Process.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>(114)</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Sixth AP Fund</h6>
<p>Sandberg acknowledges that defining an investment universe is something that they are<br>still trying to deal with. The Fund also realises that they have been criticised for investing<br>in companies in the south-west of Sweden and they take pains to show visitors that their<br>interests lie all over the country - especially in the funds in which they have a joint<br>venture relationship.</p>
<p>Since 1997 was effectively the first year of start-up, the initial problem was wading<br>through the flood of requests from smaller companies. Sandberg believes that the<br>problem of defining an “investment universe” is rather more narrowing down the requests<br>that have come in as well as actively seeking out candidates that the Fund itself is<br>interested in. The final issue is that the Fund is determined to act in a commercial<br>manner, investing in companies with an IRR of 15%-plus depending on perceived risk.<br>They do not view themselves as an agency designed to pass out govemment subsidies<br>although they occasionally run into company management who believe that this is their<br>mission.</p>
<p>Fredrik Karlsson is responsible for the first level of screening and also for managing all<br>the transactions involved with the deal including screening proposals, negotiation and<br>closing contracts. Besides reading the formal proposal, Karlsson screens for companies in<br>interesting sectors or businesses, seemingly sharing something of a preference for top-line<br>growth with his equity colleagues, although he claims that retums from cost-cutting<br>measures are potentially just as interesting.</p>
<p>The second part of the investment process involves company analysis along with trying to<br>work out whether there will be a meeting of the minds with existing management on<br>strategic direction, management of the company and the deal price. Karlsson believes it is<br>good to start talking about price at an early stage as deals have collapsed at the eleventh<br>hour for no reason other than price. To begin, the Fund want to choose companies in<br>business areas that they think are attractive. They want to understand the industry<br>prospects as well as the company’s competitive positioning within it. For technical areas<br>such as technology and life Sciences, the industry specialists on the team take a view on<br>whether or not the company’s products have a chance of being successful; academics<br>known to the Fund are sometimes also employed in a Consulting role to assess this<br>aspect.</p>
<p>The next step - ownership process - is to come to a meeting of the minds with existing<br>management about the strategic direction and management style of the company. Why<br>are existing management seeking further Capital? Do they want to expand an existing<br>business, develop a new one, or are they eventually looking to sell the company? The<br>Fund tends to prefer companies who eventually want to be listed on the stock exchange,<br>but they may consider the latter kind of company from time to time. A significant amount<br>of due diligence is required at this stage and at the company analysis stage and these two<br>processes take anywhere between three to six months.</p>
<p>The final step - exit strategy - is simply about knowing where, when, why and how the<br>Fund intends to exit the company. As stated previously, the Fund wish to favour<br>companies where the exit strategy will be to list on the Stock Exchange as this is part of<br>their mission.</p>
<p>Karlsson also talked about bit about the price at which the fund will take an initial stake<br>in a company, at the end of stage three. He stated that the majority of deals that they did</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>10 Riksdagen 1997/98. 1 saml. Nr 163</p>
<p>[115]</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Sixth AP Fund</h6>
<p>during 1997 had an IRR between 15% and 30%, with the lower IRRs, obviously, taken in<br>the companies which they perceive to be less risky. These usually involve companies<br>expanding existing business in a fairly steady industry. Karlsson stated that all the “Life<br>Science” deals they did in 1997 involved companies “in the red” who are developing<br>products; these were done at a higher IRR. Risk is assessed on a qualitative basis by the<br>Fund investment professionals involved in the deal.</p>
<p>The Private Equity team meets weekly on a Monday moming for two and a half hours to<br>go through the status of all the on-going projects and any particular issues associated with<br>them. This includes a discussion of new deals that Karlsson has screened and a “SWOT”<br>discussion on new deals being assigned at that meeting. Generally, two people are<br>assigned to do a preliminary company analysis before a larger team gets involved in the<br>legal “ownership” process. Before any deal is consummated, it needs formal approval by<br>the Board and before presentation to the board, it will be passed through the Investment<br>Committee (Sandberg, Karlsson &amp; Gustafsson).</p>
<p>7.5.7 Asset Management</p>
<p>Asset management is the term the Fund uses for traditional investment in listed securities,<br>both in fixed income and in equity. When the Sixth Fund received its initial funding from<br>the First through Third Funds, the majority was placed in fixed income securities and in<br>equities. This initial step was for safekeeping as the private equity activities were built<br>up. However, the Sixth Fund intends to continue to invest in traditional listed markets in<br>order to build up it’s “pot” of money to be used for private equity purposes. As of the<br>end of 1999, the intention is to have about 10% of the funds invested in private equity,<br>30% in fixed income and 60% in equities.</p>
<p>Asset Allocation</p>
<p>The Fund views fixed income, equities and cash as alternative asset classes. Asset<br>allocation decisions are taken actively on a monthly basis. Decision makers include Peter<br>Sandberg, Fredrik Karlsson, Erling Gustafsson, Annika Ahl and the asset managers Fredrik<br>Magni and Henrik Malmsten. The asset allocation decision goes hand in hand with the<br>outlook for the fixed income and equity markets.</p>
<p>Fixed Income</p>
<p>The Fund takes its own view on the economy and on the direction of interest rates.<br>Inputs are not internal, rather the Fund uses extemal broker research for inputs and takes<br>its own view based on that extemal research. This forms the basis for the fixed income<br>investment policy (and asset allocation). The Fund attempts to add value to fixed income<br>portfolios by taking a duration position vis-å-vis the index which can vary by plus or<br>minus one “year” against the OMRX benchmark index (which has a duration of about 3-8<br>years). Once the duration position has been decided, an implementation plan is drawn<br>up and reviewed on a weekly basis; there are no formal requirements to exercise a<br>strategy within a particular amount of time, but in practice most of the decisions are<br>implemented on a timely basis.</p>
<p>Since October 1997, the fund has taken a negative view on the direction of Swedish<br>interest rates. This view has been based both on the Outlook for economic fundamentals<br>and on perceived valuation of the market. Basically, the team do not believe that the<br>spread between Swedish and German interest rates can get much narrower. The fund has</p>

<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Sixth AP Fund</h6>
<p>underperformed the index since October, based on their short duration position vis-ä-vis<br>the index. However, they are sticking with this short position.</p>
<p>Fredrik Magni is the Fixed Income Portfolio Manager responsible for the performance of<br>the fixed income portfolio. He is assisted by Mats Venndahl, who also assists the equity<br>portfolio manager. Magni holds a Master of Science in Business Administration and has<br>five years of asset management experience. He too was with Volvo Group Finance prior<br>to his employment at the Sixth Fund. Note that I did not get a chance to speak directly<br>with Magni, who was on holiday.</p>
<p>Equities</p>
<p>Henrik Malmsten is the Equity Portfolio Manager responsible for the performance of the<br>equity portfolio. He is assisted by Mats Venndahl, who also assists the fixed income<br>portfolio manager. Malmsten has eight years asset management experience, mainly in<br>equities and was previously managing Nordic equities at Codan Bank in Denmark; prior<br>to that, he worked in asset management at Swedebank. Malmsten is currently managing<br>approximately SKr. 3.2 billion in equity assets.</p>
<p>Malmsten takes his macro-economic view from the monthly asset allocation meeting and<br>this helps to set investment themes at the sectoral level. The first decision is whether or<br>not to over- or under-weight one of the seven broad sectors and the second decision is<br>stock-picking within the sector. Intemal guidelines restrict sector over- and under-<br>weightings to plus or minus 7 percentage points. Malmsten is allowed to deviate up to 3<br>percentage points on his own authority with President Peter Sandberg approving a ftirther<br>4 percentage points against the index. By law, the Fund is also prohibited from taking<br>more than a 30% stake in any listed company, with the exception of strategic holdings<br>(see end of this section). The board has decided a limit of 10%.</p>
<p>Malmsten has a preference for “quality growth” stocks over the long term, however he<br>takes a pragmatic view toward portfolio management in light of the Swedish index which<br>also contains cyclical stocks. All things being equal, he prefers to buy companies with<br>good management and good top-line growth who are able to grow earnings over the<br>longer term.</p>
<p>Research comes from the brokers and the Fund uses about twenty Swedish brokers, out<br>of which five brokers are used on a regular basis. Malmsten tries to target the analysts he<br>feels are best in each sector to give inputs into stock research; he feels he knows who<br>the good brokers are from his previous experience. He also pointed out that, because<br>they are one of the govemment funds, companies are quite keen to come to them to visit.<br>This is particularly true of some of the small and medium-sized companies who believe<br>that they might be able to get some of the strategic equity money. Malmsten also pointed<br>out that it is their practice that when companies come to visit, he will be joined by one of<br>the Private Equity team - some of whom have significant experience in investing.</p>
<p>The Fund uses the Affärsvärlden index as its benchmark for equities. Malmsten pointed<br>out that because the Fund is not allowed to buy any company which has a non-Swedish<br>parent, he is precluded from buying shares such as Pharmacia/Upjohn, Nokia and Autoliv,<br>all of which are in the index, but are non-Swedish (Nokia) or have a non-Swedish parent<br>(Pharmacia/Upjohn &amp; Autoliv). This introduces a degree of risk against benchmark over<br>which the Fund has no control.</p>
<p>Manager Research</p>
<p>[117]</p>
<p>1998</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<h6>Sixth AP Fund</h6>
<p>Malmsten spoke briefly about the initial funding of the equity portfolio. Initially, they<br>bought baskets of stocks which represented the index (excluding the non-approved<br>shares). These were not done as programme trades, but were rather done manually.<br>They also put SKr. 100 million into a Swedish small cap fund and then gradually sold this<br>fund down to finance their own investments in mid-caps.</p>
<p>Strategic Holdings</p>
<p>This is an asset class which falls between Asset Management and Private Equities. Within<br>the Strategic Holdings portfolio, the Fund takes a long-term management stake in small,<br>illiquid, listed companies. The holdings are chosen by Malmsten and the appropriate<br>expert on the Private Equity side; in many cases, the latter individual may lead the<br>process as the skills are often more analogous to Private Equity. The Fund will not take a<br>stake in any company which does not accept the Fund as a partner in long-term strategic<br>management. The Fund may hold up to 30% of total equity in a company classified as a<br>strategic holding.</p>
<p>7.5.8 Risk Management</p>
<p>Annika Ahl is the Risk Manager and it is her job to monitor portfolio risk, to make certain<br>that the investment professionals stay within accepted guidelines and to devise new ways<br>of monitoring portfolio risk. One of her biggest challenges is to develop a way to<br>monitor the risk of the entire portfolio of private equity plus fixed income plus equities.<br>The Fund hopes to develop a way to measure Value At Risk for the private equity<br>portfolio with the help of academia.</p>
<p>In December 1997, the fund installed TMS 2000 system which has been developed by the<br>Danish company Simcorp. This is an integrated front and back office IT system which<br>also incorporates risk management tools. It is being used by quite a few Swedish fund<br>management organisations as it is a cost-effective tool for “smaller” asset management<br>firms. It is an alternative to more well-known systems such as Portia which are<br>prohibitively expensive for smaller managers.</p>
<p>Ahl monitors that portfolio managers stay within the specified fund guidelines, be they<br>legal requirements or self-imposed intemal guidelines. She also provides information to<br>the fund managers as to the positioning of the in-house funds vis-å-vis the selected<br>benchmark. These help the portfolio managers see where they are taking their bets, both<br>positive and negative. Daily, weekly and monthly risk reports are produced along with<br>the reports produced for the Board.</p>

<h5>Appendix 3</h5>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Förklaringar och definitioner</p>
<table border="1">
<tr><td>
<p>Effektiv årsavkastning</p></td><td>
<p>mäter genomsnittlig avkastning uttryckt i<br>årstakt. Medelvärdet av den månatliga<br>avkastningen, R<sub>min</sub> (i %), räknas om till årstakt,<br>R<sub>lr</sub> (i %), med hjälp av följande formel:</p>
<p>R<sub>ä</sub> = ( (l+R<sub>Mn</sub>/100)<sup>12</sup>-l)’100</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Överavkastning</p></td><td>
<p>är portföljens avkastning minus avkastningen för<br>benchmark alt för den riskfria räntan.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Standardavvikelse</p></td><td>
<p>är ett riskmått som beräknas genom att summera<br>varje observations avvikelse från medelvärdet i<br>kvadrat, dividerat med antalet observationer.<br>Standardavvikelsen är lika med roten ur detta tal,<br>dvs roten ur den genomsnittliga avvikelsen i<br>kvadrat. Ju större spridning i avkastningen, desto<br>högre standardavvikelse.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Årlig volatilitet</p></td><td>
<p>är ett sätt att uttrycka risken. Volatiliteten är i vårt<br>fall lika med standardavvikelsen - då<br>observationerna är månadsnoteringar -<br>multiplicerat med kvadratroten ur tolv, dvs<br>standardavvikelsen i årstakt.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Alfavärde</p></td><td>
<p>beskriver hur mycket en akties (eller<br>aktieportföljs) värde i genomsnitt förändras då<br>hela aktiemarknadens värde är oförändrat. Anges<br>i procent per år och beräknas utifrån den<br>historiska utvecklingen. Jämför med betavärdet<br>som beskriver hur mycket samma värde (utöver<br>alfavärdet) förändras då hela aktiemarknaden<br>förändras.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Betavärde</p></td><td>
<p>mäter marknadsrisken, d v s en akties (eller<br>aktieportföljs) benägenhet att förändras i pris när<br>hela aktiemarknaden ändras. Betavärdet är ett<br>historiskt värde som beräknas genom en<br>jämförelse av aktiens förändring och hela<br>aktiemarknadens förändring över en och samma<br>tidsperiod. Vid betavärde 1,0 råder lika följsamhet<br>med hela aktiemarknaden. En väl diversifierad<br>aktieportfölj (t ex en fond) med betavärdet 1,0<br>bör ha ungefär samma standardavvikelse som<br>hela marknaden.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Sharpe's kvot</p></td><td>
<p>visar den riskjusterade avkastningen då risk mäts<br>som standardavvikelse i årstakt.</p>
<p>Överavkastningen mot den riskfria räntan (R<sub>p</sub> - R,)<br>divideras med standardavvikelsen (stdVp):</p></td></tr>
</table>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<table border="1">
<tr><td></td><td>
<p>R<sub>P</sub>-R<sub>f</sub></p>
<p>SR-</p>
<p>stdv<sub>p</sub></p>
<p>Noteras bör att då portföljens avkastning är lägre<br>än den antagna räntan premieras portföljer med<br>höga standardavvikelser. Av denna anledning har<br>vi valt att utelämna Sharpe's kvot vid negativa<br>värden.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Treynor's kvot</p></td><td>
<p>visar den riskjusterade avkastningen då risk mäts<br>som beta. Överavkastningen mot den riskfria<br>räntan (Rp-R,) divideras med betavärdet (beta^:</p>
<p>Rp-R<sub>f</sub></p>
<p>TR„ ------------</p>
<p>beta</p>
<p>p</p>
<p>Noteras bör att då portföljens avkastning är lägre<br>än den antagna räntan premieras portföljer med<br>höga beta värden. Av denna anledning har vi valt<br>att utelämna Treynor's kvot vid negativa värden.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Tracking error</p></td><td>
<p>är ett statistiskt riskmått som beräknar aktiens<br>(aktieportföljens) kursutvecklingens<br>standardavvikelse runt benchmarks utveckling.<br>TE visar med 68 procents sannolikhet gränserna<br>för en procentuella avvikelsen från index som<br>kan förväntas i framtiden, baserat på historiska<br>data.</p>
<p>TE=stdv(R<sub>p</sub> - R<sub>bm</sub>)</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Information Ratio</p></td><td>
<p>mäter den riskjusteradé avkastning uttryckt som<br>överavkastningen mot jämförelseindex dividerat<br>med Tracking Error. En positiv värde innebär att<br>den extra risken förvaltaren tagit har genererat en<br>högre avkastning. Ett tal på +0,5 brukar anses<br>som godkänt, 0,75 som bra och +1,0 som<br>utmärkt.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Attribution eller<br>Bidrag</p></td><td>
<p>uttrycker tillgångsfördelningens bidrag till den<br>överavkastning som totalportföljen generar i<br>förhållande till jämförelseindex och är den del av<br>överavkastningen jämfört med benchmark som<br>beror på val av tillgångsklasser, dvs förmågan att<br>löpande välja rätt vikt i vart och ett av portföljens<br>tillgångsslag.</p></td></tr>
</table>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Engelska definitioner</p>
<table border="1">
<tr><td>
<p>Standard Deviation</p></td><td>
<p>is a measure of the absolute variability of returns.<br>Standard deviation is the most commonly used<br>measure of the volatility of returns or investment<br>risk.</p>
<p>Many people have criticised standard deviation as<br>a measure of risk as it takes no account of<br>performance compared to benchmark or<br>objectives. It also assumes that investors will be<br>equally concemed by upside and downside<br>variance. For these reasons we present some<br>other risk measures in the Mercer Sector Retums<br>Surveys as well as standard deviation.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Tracking Error</p></td><td>
<p>is a measure of a manager’s risk or volatility<br>compared to a benchmark. It is a relative<br>measure and does not attempt to measure<br>absolute return or absolute risk. In essence, it<br>measures the risk of significant departures from<br>the benchmark. It is calculated as the standard<br>deviation of excess returns.</p>
<p>A portfolio with a low tracking error will follow<br>the index closely while a portfolio with a high<br>tracking error may depart from the index<br>significantly. Mercer calculates the tracking error<br>as the standard deviation of the monthly<br>deviations from the index. This standard<br>deviation is then annualised by multiplying it by<br>root twelve.</p>
<p>Tracking error is best ttsed to evaluate managers<br>who manage portfolios directly against the index.<br>This is best thought of as a manager who starts<br>with an index portfolio and then makes specific<br>‘tilts’ or ‘bets’ away from the index. Most<br>managers these days can be classified in this<br>manner.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Information ratio</p></td><td>
<p>Tilts or bets away from the benchmark lead to<br>tracking error in the portfolio. The purpose of the<br>information ratio is to measure the success of<br>these ‘tilts’ away from the index. It takes account<br>of the fact that each of these tilts should generate<br>excess return but will also add risk to the extent<br>that they will cause the portfolio retum to deviate<br>from the index return.</p>
<p>The information ratio measures the amount of<br>‘information’ that the manager can extract from<br>the market. Expressed in another way this is the</p></td></tr>
</table>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163</p>
<p>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Beta</p>
<p>amount of excess return generated per unit of<br>risk or tracking error added.</p>
<p>One of the advantages of looking at the<br>information ratio is that the more aggressive<br>managers can be evaluated on the same basis as<br>the conservative ones. Consider two managers<br>who, with equally sized portfolios, make the<br>same bets away from the index. Manager A takes<br>bets that are double the size of manager B. If the<br>bets add value, manager A will achieve a higher<br>return and a higher ranking in the performance<br>surveys. However, the information ratios will be<br>the same for both managers. Managers should<br>not necessarily be rewarded for using the same<br>information but getting more out of it by<br>cranking up the risk.</p>
<p>The information ratio is therefore a measure of<br>the skill of the manager. If the information ratio<br>is large and it is measured over a reasonable<br>period of time, then this is an indication that the<br>manager has some skill in managing money. For<br>the more statistically minded, the information<br>ratio multiplied by square root of the number of<br>periods over which it was measured is a t-statistic<br>that measures the significance of the alpha.</p>
<p>It is therefore possible to define the beta as the<br>extent to which manageris returns have varied in<br>line with movements in index retums. A beta of<br>greater than 1 indicates that when the market<br>rises, this manager is likely to perform better than<br>the market. A beta of less then 1 indicates that<br>when the market rises the manager will not<br>perform as well as the market. The converse is<br>true in falling markets.</p>
<p>Beta therefore measures the sensitivity of the<br>manageris returns to the market return. Most<br>managers would tend to have a beta of around 1,<br>however, a low beta in a period of falling<br>markets or a high beta in a rising market would<br>explain an outperformance by the manager.</p>
<p>It should be noted that a manageris beta will<br>change over time. A manager that is trying to<br>‘time’ the market will change the beta of the<br>portfolio in response to expected market<br>conditions. Therefore, the implicit assumption of<br>the regression analysis, that beta is constant over<br>the period, may be flawed.</p>
<p>Alpha</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>is a residual addition to the manager’s return. A<br>positive alpha would imply that the manager<br>was able to add value through sources other than<br>taking on market risk. The major source of<br>‘alpha’ would be stock selection.</p>
<p>Alpha is therefore used as a basis for determining<br>whether the manager has any skill in managing<br>money in that asset class. It is the expected<br>excess retum estimated on the basis of statistical<br>analysis of past results. Note that the alpha may<br>vary from the historic excess retum because it<br>makes an allowance for the historic level of risk.</p>
<p>Skr. 1997/98:163<br>Bilaga 5</p>
<p>Definitioner till stilanalysen</p>
<table border="1">
<tr><td>
<p>Value</p></td><td>
<p>A measure intended to assess the inherent worth<br>of equity shares. Considerable research indicates<br>that “Value shares” tend to outperform over the<br>longer term. However, there appear also to be<br>long, regular, periods of sustained<br>underperformance of Value shares. Typically,<br>Value is assessed using Book to Price (book<br>value per share, divided by share price), or using<br>Dividend Yield, but there are other related<br>criteria which offer useful additional information<br>within many global markets.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Growth</p></td><td>
<p>Growth stocks are often considered as simply<br>those securities which are rated low on the<br>standard Value scale. They have too high a price<br>relative to their book value and pay out too<br>meagre a dividend to be considered as Value<br>stocks. These securities must, so it is said, have<br>higher growth prospects than typical Value<br>stocks, in order to justify their higher prices. This<br>definition is rather narrow, however, there are a<br>number of other criteria which offer further<br>profitable insights into the nature of corporate<br>growth and equity growth potential.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Size/Risk</p></td><td>
<p>Large company securities perform differently<br>from small company securities, in fact most<br>research indicates that small company shares<br>outperform large company shares over the<br>medium term, compensating investors for their<br>additional risk. Nonetheless, large company<br>shares frequently outperform over long and<br>sustained periods, when equity markets are not<br>adequately rewarding risk taking. There is more<br>to the “Size Effect” than market capitalization<br>alone.</p></td></tr>
<tr><td>
<p>Record</p></td><td>
<p>Even the “Weak Form” of the “Efficient Market<br>Hypothesis” States that past share price<br>performance offers no assistance in predicting<br>future price performance, but still many chartists<br>do manage to make convincing gains. The Global<br>Style Advisor attempts to gain an insight into the<br>world of the “technical analyst” by reviewing the<br>history of success of a simple share price relative<br>performance theme.</p></td></tr>
</table>
